<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Study of the structure of organisms and their parts</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the anatomy of plants, see <a href="/wiki/Plant_anatomy" title="Plant anatomy">Plant anatomy</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Anatomy_(disambiguation)" title="Anatomy (disambiguation)">Anatomy (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p><b>Anatomy</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%80%CE%BD%CE%B1%CF%84%CE%BF%CE%BC%CE%AE#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:ἀνατομή">ἀνατομή</a></span><i> (<span lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>anatomḗ</i></span>)</i> '<a href="/wiki/Dissection" title="Dissection">dissection</a>') is the branch of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a> concerned with the study of the structure of organisms and their parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Anatomy is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural science</a> that deals with the structural organization of living things. It is an old science, having its beginnings in prehistoric times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Anatomy is inherently tied to <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">developmental biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Embryology" title="Embryology">embryology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comparative_anatomy" title="Comparative anatomy">comparative anatomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_biology" title="Evolutionary biology">evolutionary biology</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_HGray_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-intro_HGray-3">[3]</a></sup> as these are the processes by which anatomy is generated, both over immediate and long-term timescales. Anatomy and <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>, which study the structure and <a href="/wiki/Function_(biology)" title="Function (biology)">function</a> of organisms and their parts respectively, make a natural pair of related disciplines, and are often studied together. Human anatomy is one of the essential <a href="/wiki/Basic_research" title="Basic research">basic sciences</a> that are applied in medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The discipline of anatomy is divided into <a href="/wiki/Macroscopic_scale" title="Macroscopic scale">macroscopic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microscopic_scale" title="Microscopic scale">microscopic</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gross_anatomy" title="Gross anatomy">Macroscopic anatomy</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Gross_anatomy" title="Gross anatomy">gross anatomy</a>, is the examination of an animal's body parts using unaided eyesight. Gross anatomy also includes the branch of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superficial_anatomy" title="Superficial anatomy">superficial anatomy</a>. Microscopic anatomy involves the use of optical instruments in the study of the <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissues</a> of various structures, known as <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">histology</a>, and also in the study of <a href="/wiki/Cell_biology" title="Cell biology">cells</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_anatomy" title="History of anatomy">history of anatomy</a> is characterized by a progressive understanding of the functions of the organs and structures of the human body. Methods have also improved dramatically, advancing from the examination of animals by <a href="/wiki/Dissection" title="Dissection">dissection</a> of carcasses and <a href="/wiki/Cadaver" title="Cadaver">cadavers</a> (corpses) to 20th century <a href="/wiki/Medical_imaging" title="Medical imaging">medical imaging</a> techniques, including <a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">X-ray</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultrasound_imaging" title="Ultrasound imaging">ultrasound</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">magnetic resonance imaging</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_Definition">Etymology and Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology and Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:An_%C3%A9corch%C3%A9_figure_(life-size),_lying_prone_on_a_table_Wellcome_L0020561.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3590" data-file-width="4985" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/An_%C3%A9corch%C3%A9_figure_%28life-size%29%2C_lying_prone_on_a_table_Wellcome_L0020561.jpg/220px-An_%C3%A9corch%C3%A9_figure_%28life-size%29%2C_lying_prone_on_a_table_Wellcome_L0020561.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/An_%C3%A9corch%C3%A9_figure_%28life-size%29%2C_lying_prone_on_a_table_Wellcome_L0020561.jpg/330px-An_%C3%A9corch%C3%A9_figure_%28life-size%29%2C_lying_prone_on_a_table_Wellcome_L0020561.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/An_%C3%A9corch%C3%A9_figure_%28life-size%29%2C_lying_prone_on_a_table_Wellcome_L0020561.jpg/440px-An_%C3%A9corch%C3%A9_figure_%28life-size%29%2C_lying_prone_on_a_table_Wellcome_L0020561.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:An_%C3%A9corch%C3%A9_figure_(life-size),_lying_prone_on_a_table_Wellcome_L0020561.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A dissected body, lying prone on a table, by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Landseer" title="Charles Landseer">Charles Landseer</a></div></div></div>
<p>Derived from the Greek <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἀνατομή</span></span> <i>anatomē</i> "dissection" (from <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἀνατέμνω</span></span> <i>anatémnō</i> "I cut up, cut open" from ἀνά <i>aná</i> "up," and τέμνω <i>témnō</i> "I cut"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> anatomy is the scientific study of the structure of <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> including their systems, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organs" title="Organs">Organ (biology)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissues</a>. It includes the appearance and position of the various parts, the materials from which they are composed, and their relationships with other parts. Anatomy is quite distinct from <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>, which deal respectively with the functions of those parts and the chemical processes involved. For example, an anatomist is concerned with the shape, size, position, structure, blood supply and innervation of an organ such as the liver; while a physiologist is interested in the production of <a href="/wiki/Bile" title="Bile">bile</a>, the role of the liver in nutrition and the regulation of bodily functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Everyman_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Everyman-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The discipline of anatomy can be subdivided into a number of branches, including gross or <a href="/wiki/Macroscopic_scale" title="Macroscopic scale">macroscopic</a> anatomy and <a href="/wiki/Microscopic_scale" title="Microscopic scale">microscopic</a> anatomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gross_anatomy" title="Gross anatomy">Gross anatomy</a> is the study of structures large enough to be seen with the naked eye, and also includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superficial_anatomy" title="Superficial anatomy">superficial anatomy</a> or surface anatomy, the study by sight of the external body features. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microscopic_anatomy" title="Microscopic anatomy">Microscopic anatomy</a> is the study of structures on a microscopic scale, along with <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">histology</a> (the study of tissues), and <a href="/wiki/Embryology" title="Embryology">embryology</a> (the study of an organism in its immature condition).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_HGray_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-intro_HGray-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Anatomy can be studied using both invasive and non-invasive methods with the goal of obtaining information about the structure and organization of organs and systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_HGray_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-intro_HGray-3">[3]</a></sup> Methods used include <a href="/wiki/Dissection" title="Dissection">dissection</a>, in which a body is opened and its organs studied, and <a href="/wiki/Endoscopy" title="Endoscopy">endoscopy</a>, in which a <a href="/wiki/Video_camera" title="Video camera">video camera</a>-equipped instrument is inserted through a small incision in the body wall and used to explore the internal organs and other structures. <a href="/wiki/Angiography" title="Angiography">Angiography</a> using <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> or <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_angiography" title="Magnetic resonance angiography">magnetic resonance angiography</a> are methods to visualize blood vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "anatomy" is commonly taken to refer to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_anatomy" title="Human anatomy">human anatomy</a>. However, substantially similar structures and tissues are found throughout the rest of the animal kingdom, and the term also includes the anatomy of other animals. The term <i>zootomy</i> is also sometimes used to specifically refer to non-human animals. The structure and tissues of plants are of a dissimilar nature and they are studied in <a href="/wiki/Plant_anatomy" title="Plant anatomy">plant anatomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Everyman_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Everyman-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_tissues">Animal tissues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Animal tissues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anima_cell_notext.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="374" data-file-width="579" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Anima_cell_notext.svg/220px-Anima_cell_notext.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Anima_cell_notext.svg/330px-Anima_cell_notext.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Anima_cell_notext.svg/440px-Anima_cell_notext.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anima_cell_notext.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stylized cutaway diagram of an animal cell (with flagella)</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">kingdom</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animalia" title="Animalia">Animalia</a> contains <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">multicellular organisms</a> that are <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">heterotrophic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Motility" title="Motility">motile</a> (although some have secondarily adopted a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sessility_(zoology)" title="Sessility (zoology)">sessile</a> lifestyle). Most animals have bodies differentiated into separate <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissues</a> and these animals are also known as <a href="/wiki/Eumetazoa" title="Eumetazoa">eumetazoans</a>. They have an internal <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestive</a> chamber, with one or two openings; the <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gametes</a> are produced in multicellular sex organs, and the <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">zygotes</a> include a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blastula" title="Blastula">blastula</a> stage in their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryogenesis" title="Embryogenesis">embryonic development</a>. Metazoans do not include the <a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">sponges</a>, which have undifferentiated cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit549_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit549-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike <a href="/wiki/Plant_cell" title="Plant cell">plant cells</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_cells" title="Animal cells">animal cells</a> have neither a cell wall nor <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplasts</a>. Vacuoles, when present, are more in number and much smaller than those in the plant cell. The body tissues are composed of numerous types of cell, including those found in muscles, nerves and skin. Each typically has a cell membrane formed of <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a>.  All of the different cells of an animal are derived from the embryonic <a href="/wiki/Germ_layer" title="Germ layer">germ layers</a>. Those simpler invertebrates which are formed from two germ layers of ectoderm and endoderm are called <a href="/wiki/Diploblasty" title="Diploblasty">diploblastic</a> and the more developed animals whose structures and organs are formed from three germ layers are called <a href="/wiki/Triploblasty" title="Triploblasty">triploblastic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert60_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert60-13">[13]</a></sup> All of a triploblastic animal's tissues and organs are derived from the three germ layers of the embryo, the <a href="/wiki/Ectoderm" title="Ectoderm">ectoderm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesoderm" title="Mesoderm">mesoderm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Endoderm" title="Endoderm">endoderm</a>.
</p><p>Animal tissues can be grouped into four basic types: <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epithelium" title="Epithelium">epithelial</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscle_tissue" title="Muscle tissue">muscle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nervous_tissue" title="Nervous tissue">nervous tissue</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cartilage_polarised.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Cartilage_polarised.jpg/220px-Cartilage_polarised.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Cartilage_polarised.jpg/330px-Cartilage_polarised.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Cartilage_polarised.jpg/440px-Cartilage_polarised.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cartilage_polarised.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hyaline cartilage at high magnification (<a href="/wiki/H%26E_stain" title="H&amp;E stain">H&amp;E stain</a>)</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Connective_tissue">Connective tissue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Connective tissue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">Connective tissues</a> are fibrous and made up of cells scattered among inorganic material called the <a href="/wiki/Extracellular_matrix" title="Extracellular matrix">extracellular matrix</a>. Connective tissue gives shape to organs and holds them in place. The main types are loose connective tissue, <a href="/wiki/Adipose_tissue" title="Adipose tissue">adipose tissue</a>, fibrous connective tissue, <a href="/wiki/Cartilage" title="Cartilage">cartilage</a> and bone. The extracellular matrix contains <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, the chief and most abundant of which is <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a>. Collagen plays a major part in organizing and maintaining tissues. The matrix can be modified to form a skeleton to support or protect the body. An <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">exoskeleton</a> is a thickened, rigid <a href="/wiki/Cuticle" title="Cuticle">cuticle</a> which is stiffened by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mineralisation_(biology)" title="Mineralisation (biology)">mineralization</a>, as in <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> or by the cross-linking of its proteins as in <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>. An <a href="/wiki/Endoskeleton" title="Endoskeleton">endoskeleton</a> is internal and present in all developed animals, as well as in many of those less developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert60_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert60-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epithelium">Epithelium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Epithelium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Normal_gastric_mucosa_low_mag.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Normal_gastric_mucosa_low_mag.jpg/220px-Normal_gastric_mucosa_low_mag.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Normal_gastric_mucosa_low_mag.jpg/330px-Normal_gastric_mucosa_low_mag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Normal_gastric_mucosa_low_mag.jpg/440px-Normal_gastric_mucosa_low_mag.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Normal_gastric_mucosa_low_mag.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gastric mucosa at low magnification (<a href="/wiki/H%26E_stain" title="H&amp;E stain">H&amp;E stain</a>)</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epithelial_tissue" title="Epithelial tissue">Epithelial tissue</a> is composed of closely packed cells, bound to each other by <a href="/wiki/Cell_adhesion_molecule" title="Cell adhesion molecule">cell adhesion molecules</a>, with little intercellular space. Epithelial cells can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squamous_epithelial_cell" title="Squamous epithelial cell">squamous</a> (flat), <a href="/wiki/Simple_cuboidal_epithelium" title="Simple cuboidal epithelium">cuboidal</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columnar_epithelial_cell" title="Columnar epithelial cell">columnar</a> and rest on a <a href="/wiki/Basal_lamina" title="Basal lamina">basal lamina</a>, the upper layer of the <a href="/wiki/Basement_membrane" title="Basement membrane">basement membrane</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> the lower layer is the reticular lamina lying next to the connective tissue in the extracellular matrix secreted by the epithelial cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> There are many different types of epithelium, modified to suit a particular function. In the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">respiratory tract</a> there is a type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudostratified_ciliated_columnar_epithelium" title="Pseudostratified ciliated columnar epithelium">ciliated</a> epithelial lining; in the small intestine there are <a href="/wiki/Microvillus" title="Microvillus">microvilli</a> on the epithelial lining and in the large intestine there are <a href="/wiki/Intestinal_villus" title="Intestinal villus">intestinal villi</a>. Skin consists of an outer layer of <a href="/wiki/Keratin" title="Keratin">keratinized</a> stratified squamous epithelium that covers the exterior of the vertebrate body. <a href="/wiki/Keratinocyte" title="Keratinocyte">Keratinocytes</a> make up to 95% of the cells in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epidermis_(skin)" title="Epidermis (skin)">skin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The epithelial cells on the external surface of the body typically secrete an extracellular matrix in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Cuticle" title="Cuticle">cuticle</a>. In simple animals this may just be a coat of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycoproteins" title="Glycoproteins">glycoproteins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert60_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert60-13">[13]</a></sup> In more advanced animals, many <a href="/wiki/Gland" title="Gland">glands</a> are formed of epithelial cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Muscle_tissue">Muscle tissue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Muscle tissue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skeletal_muscle_-_cross_section,_nerve_bundle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="709" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Skeletal_muscle_-_cross_section%2C_nerve_bundle.jpg/220px-Skeletal_muscle_-_cross_section%2C_nerve_bundle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Skeletal_muscle_-_cross_section%2C_nerve_bundle.jpg/330px-Skeletal_muscle_-_cross_section%2C_nerve_bundle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Skeletal_muscle_-_cross_section%2C_nerve_bundle.jpg/440px-Skeletal_muscle_-_cross_section%2C_nerve_bundle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skeletal_muscle_-_cross_section,_nerve_bundle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cross section through <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle" title="Skeletal muscle">skeletal muscle</a> and a small <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">nerve</a> at high magnification (<a href="/wiki/H%26E_stain" title="H&amp;E stain">H&amp;E stain</a>)</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle_cells" title="Muscle cells">Muscle cells</a> (myocytes) form the active contractile tissue of the body. <a href="/wiki/Muscle_tissue" title="Muscle tissue">Muscle tissue</a> functions to produce force and cause motion, either locomotion or movement within internal organs. Muscle is formed of contractile <a href="/wiki/Myofibril" title="Myofibril">filaments</a> and is separated into three main types; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smooth_muscle_tissue" title="Smooth muscle tissue">smooth muscle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skeletal_striated_muscle" title="Skeletal striated muscle">skeletal muscle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_muscle" title="Cardiac muscle">cardiac muscle</a>. Smooth muscle has no <a href="/wiki/Striated_muscle_tissue" title="Striated muscle tissue">striations</a> when examined microscopically. It contracts slowly but maintains contractibility over a wide range of stretch lengths. It is found in such organs as <a href="/wiki/Sea_anemone" title="Sea anemone">sea anemone</a> tentacles and the body wall of <a href="/wiki/Sea_cucumber" title="Sea cucumber">sea cucumbers</a>. Skeletal muscle contracts rapidly but has a limited range of extension. It is found in the movement of appendages and jaws. Obliquely striated muscle is intermediate between the other two. The filaments are staggered and this is the type of muscle found in <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">earthworms</a> that can extend slowly or make rapid contractions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert103_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert103-18">[18]</a></sup> In higher animals striated muscles occur in bundles attached to bone to provide movement and are often arranged in antagonistic sets. Smooth muscle is found in the walls of the <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">uterus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bladder" title="Bladder">bladder</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestines" title="Intestines">intestines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">stomach</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oesophagus" title="Oesophagus">oesophagus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_airways" title="Respiratory airways">respiratory airways</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_muscle" title="Cardiac muscle">Cardiac muscle</a> is found only in the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>, allowing it to contract and pump blood round the body.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nervous_tissue">Nervous tissue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Nervous tissue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Neuroanatomy" title="Neuroanatomy">Neuroanatomy</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nervous_tissue" title="Nervous tissue">Nervous tissue</a> is composed of many nerve cells known as <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> which transmit information. In some slow-moving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radial_symmetry" title="Radial symmetry">radially symmetrical</a> marine animals such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ctenophore" title="Ctenophore">ctenophores</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cnidarian" title="Cnidarian">cnidarians</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Sea_anemone" title="Sea anemone">sea anemones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a>), the nerves form a <a href="/wiki/Nerve_net" title="Nerve net">nerve net</a>, but in most animals they are organized longitudinally into bundles. In simple animals, receptor neurons in the body wall cause a local reaction to a stimulus. In more complex animals, specialized receptor cells such as <a href="/wiki/Chemoreceptor" title="Chemoreceptor">chemoreceptors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photoreceptor_cell" title="Photoreceptor cell">photoreceptors</a> are found in groups and send messages along <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_neural_network" title="Biological neural network">neural networks</a> to other parts of the organism. Neurons can be connected together in <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert104_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert104-19">[19]</a></sup> In higher animals, specialized receptors are the basis of sense organs and there is a <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> (brain and spinal cord) and a <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">peripheral nervous system</a>. The latter consists of <a href="/wiki/Sensory_neuron" title="Sensory neuron">sensory nerves</a> that transmit information from sense organs and <a href="/wiki/Motor_neuron" title="Motor neuron">motor nerves</a> that influence target organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert107_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert107-21">[21]</a></sup> The peripheral nervous system is divided into the <a href="/wiki/Somatic_nervous_system" title="Somatic nervous system">somatic nervous system</a> which conveys sensation and controls <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voluntary_muscle" title="Voluntary muscle">voluntary muscle</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">autonomic nervous system</a> which involuntarily controls <a href="/wiki/Smooth_muscle" title="Smooth muscle">smooth muscle</a>, certain glands and internal organs, including the <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">stomach</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vertebrate_anatomy">Vertebrate anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Vertebrate anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate#Anatomy_and_morphology" title="Vertebrate">Vertebrate § Anatomy and morphology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Comparative_anatomy" title="Comparative anatomy">Comparative anatomy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VolRenderShearWarp.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="533" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/VolRenderShearWarp.gif/170px-VolRenderShearWarp.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/VolRenderShearWarp.gif/255px-VolRenderShearWarp.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/VolRenderShearWarp.gif/340px-VolRenderShearWarp.gif 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VolRenderShearWarp.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mouse" title="Mouse">Mouse</a> skull</div></div></div>
<p>All <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> have a similar basic <a href="/wiki/Body_plan" title="Body plan">body plan</a> and at some point in their lives, mostly in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryogenesis" title="Embryogenesis">embryonic</a> stage, share the major <a href="/wiki/Chordate" title="Chordate">chordate</a> characteristics: a stiffening rod, the <a href="/wiki/Notochord" title="Notochord">notochord</a>; a dorsal hollow tube of nervous material, the <a href="/wiki/Neural_tube" title="Neural tube">neural tube</a>; <a href="/wiki/Pharyngeal_arch" title="Pharyngeal arch">pharyngeal arches</a>; and a tail posterior to the anus. The <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a> is protected by the <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">vertebral column</a> and is above the notochord, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gut_(anatomy)" title="Gut (anatomy)">gastrointestinal tract</a> is below it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Nervous tissue is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Ectoderm" title="Ectoderm">ectoderm</a>, connective tissues are derived from <a href="/wiki/Mesoderm" title="Mesoderm">mesoderm</a>, and gut is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Endoderm" title="Endoderm">endoderm</a>. At the posterior end is a tail which continues the spinal cord and vertebrae but not the gut. The mouth is found at the anterior end of the animal, and the <a href="/wiki/Anus" title="Anus">anus</a> at the base of the tail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The defining characteristic of a vertebrate is the <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">vertebral column</a>, formed in the development of the segmented series of <a href="/wiki/Vertebra" title="Vertebra">vertebrae</a>. In most vertebrates the notochord becomes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleus_pulposus" title="Nucleus pulposus">nucleus pulposus</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Intervertebral_disc" title="Intervertebral disc">intervertebral discs</a>. However, a few vertebrates, such as the <a href="/wiki/Sturgeon" title="Sturgeon">sturgeon</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Coelacanth" title="Coelacanth">coelacanth</a>, retain the notochord into adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gnathostomata" title="Gnathostomata">Jawed vertebrates</a> are typified by paired appendages, fins or legs, which may be secondarily lost. The limbs of vertebrates are considered to be <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">homologous</a> because the same underlying skeletal structure was inherited from their last common ancestor. This is one of the arguments put forward by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> to support his theory of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fish_anatomy">Fish anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fish anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fish_anatomy" title="Fish anatomy">Fish anatomy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Internal_anatomy_of_a_fish_(cutaway_diagram).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="620" data-file-width="886" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Internal_anatomy_of_a_fish_%28cutaway_diagram%29.jpg/220px-Internal_anatomy_of_a_fish_%28cutaway_diagram%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Internal_anatomy_of_a_fish_%28cutaway_diagram%29.jpg/330px-Internal_anatomy_of_a_fish_%28cutaway_diagram%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Internal_anatomy_of_a_fish_%28cutaway_diagram%29.jpg/440px-Internal_anatomy_of_a_fish_%28cutaway_diagram%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Internal_anatomy_of_a_fish_(cutaway_diagram).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cutaway diagram showing various organs of a fish</div></div></div>
<p>The body of a fish is divided into a head, trunk and tail, although the divisions between the three are not always externally visible. The skeleton, which forms the support structure inside the fish, is either made of cartilage, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartilaginous_fish" title="Cartilaginous fish">cartilaginous fish</a>, or bone in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bony_fish" title="Bony fish">bony fish</a>. The main skeletal element is the vertebral column, composed of articulating <a href="/wiki/Vertebra" title="Vertebra">vertebrae</a> which are lightweight yet strong. The ribs attach to the spine and there are no <a href="/wiki/Limb_(anatomy)" title="Limb (anatomy)">limbs</a> or limb girdles. The main external features of the fish, the <a href="/wiki/Fish_fin" title="Fish fin">fins</a>, are composed of either bony or soft spines called rays, which with the exception of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caudal_fin" title="Caudal fin">caudal fins</a>, have no direct connection with the spine. They are supported by the muscles which compose the main part of the trunk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit818_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit818-27">[27]</a></sup> The heart has two chambers and pumps the blood through the respiratory surfaces of the <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a> and on round the body in a single circulatory loop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The eyes are adapted for seeing underwater and have only local vision. There is an inner ear but no external or <a href="/wiki/Middle_ear" title="Middle ear">middle ear</a>. Low frequency vibrations are detected by the <a href="/wiki/Lateral_line" title="Lateral line">lateral line</a> system of sense organs that run along the length of the sides of fish, and these respond to nearby movements and to changes in water pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit818_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit818-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sharks and rays are <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> fish with numerous <a href="/wiki/Primitive_(phylogenetics)" title="Primitive (phylogenetics)">primitive</a> anatomical features similar to those of ancient fish, including skeletons composed of cartilage. Their bodies tend to be dorso-ventrally flattened, they usually have five pairs of gill slits and a large mouth set on the underside of the head. The dermis is covered with separate dermal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placoid_scales" title="Placoid scales">placoid scales</a>. They have a <a href="/wiki/Cloaca" title="Cloaca">cloaca</a> into which the urinary and genital passages open, but not a <a href="/wiki/Swim_bladder" title="Swim bladder">swim bladder</a>. Cartilaginous fish produce a small number of large, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_yolk" title="Egg yolk">yolky</a> eggs. Some species are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovoviviparous" title="Ovoviviparous">ovoviviparous</a> and the young develop internally but others are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oviparous" title="Oviparous">oviparous</a> and the larvae develop externally in egg cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kotpal_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kotpal-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>The bony fish lineage shows more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Derived_trait" title="Derived trait">derived</a> anatomical traits, often with major evolutionary changes from the features of ancient fish. They have a bony skeleton, are generally laterally flattened, have five pairs of gills protected by an <a href="/wiki/Operculum_(fish)" title="Operculum (fish)">operculum</a>, and a mouth at or near the tip of the snout. The dermis is covered with overlapping <a href="/wiki/Fish_scale" title="Fish scale">scales</a>. Bony fish have a swim bladder which helps them maintain a constant depth in the water column, but not a cloaca. They mostly <a href="/wiki/Spawn_(biology)" title="Spawn (biology)">spawn</a> a large number of small eggs with little yolk which they broadcast into the water column.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kotpal_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kotpal-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amphibian_anatomy">Amphibian anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Amphibian anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphibian_anatomy" title="Amphibian anatomy">Amphibian anatomy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg"><img alt="Frog skeleton" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg/220px-Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg/330px-Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg/440px-Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ceratophrys_cornuta_skeleton_front.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skeleton of <a href="/wiki/Surinam_horned_frog" title="Surinam horned frog">Surinam horned frog</a> (<i>Ceratophrys cornuta</i>)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1668" data-file-width="1248" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG/170px-Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG/255px-Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG/340px-Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frog_anatomy_tags.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plastic model of a frog</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">Amphibians</a> are a <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">class</a> of animals comprising <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salamander" title="Salamander">salamanders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caecilian" title="Caecilian">caecilians</a>. They are <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a>, but the caecilians and a few species of salamander have either no limbs or their limbs are much reduced in size. Their main bones are hollow and lightweight and are fully ossified and the vertebrae interlock with each other and have <a href="/wiki/Articular_processes" title="Articular processes">articular processes</a>. Their ribs are usually short and may be fused to the vertebrae. Their skulls are mostly broad and short, and are often incompletely ossified. Their skin contains little <a href="/wiki/Keratin" title="Keratin">keratin</a> and lacks scales, but contains many <a href="/wiki/Mucous_gland" title="Mucous gland">mucous glands</a> and in some species, poison glands. The hearts of amphibians have three chambers, two <a href="/wiki/Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)">atria</a> and one <a href="/wiki/Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)">ventricle</a>. They have a <a href="/wiki/Urinary_bladder" title="Urinary bladder">urinary bladder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_waste#nitrogen_wastes" title="Metabolic waste">nitrogenous waste products</a> are excreted primarily as <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>. Amphibians breathe by means of <a href="/wiki/Buccal_pumping" title="Buccal pumping">buccal pumping</a>, a pump action in which air is first drawn into the <a href="/wiki/Buccopharyngeal_membrane" title="Buccopharyngeal membrane">buccopharyngeal</a> region through the nostrils. These are then closed and the air is forced into the lungs by contraction of the throat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> They supplement this with <a href="/wiki/Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange">gas exchange</a> through the skin which needs to be kept moist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit859_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit859-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>In frogs the pelvic girdle is robust and the hind legs are much longer and stronger than the forelimbs. The feet have four or five digits and the toes are often webbed for swimming or have suction pads for climbing. Frogs have large eyes and no tail. Salamanders resemble lizards in appearance; their short legs project sideways, the belly is close to or in contact with the ground and they have a long tail. Caecilians superficially resemble <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">earthworms</a> and are limbless. They burrow by means of zones of muscle contractions which move along the body and they swim by undulating their body from side to side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reptile_anatomy">Reptile anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Reptile anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reptile_anatomy" title="Reptile anatomy">Reptile anatomy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crotalus_atrox_-Museum_of_Osteology,_Oklahoma_City,_Oklahoma,_USA-29Aug2012.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3729" data-file-width="5557" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Crotalus_atrox_-Museum_of_Osteology%2C_Oklahoma_City%2C_Oklahoma%2C_USA-29Aug2012.jpg/220px-Crotalus_atrox_-Museum_of_Osteology%2C_Oklahoma_City%2C_Oklahoma%2C_USA-29Aug2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Crotalus_atrox_-Museum_of_Osteology%2C_Oklahoma_City%2C_Oklahoma%2C_USA-29Aug2012.jpg/330px-Crotalus_atrox_-Museum_of_Osteology%2C_Oklahoma_City%2C_Oklahoma%2C_USA-29Aug2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Crotalus_atrox_-Museum_of_Osteology%2C_Oklahoma_City%2C_Oklahoma%2C_USA-29Aug2012.jpg/440px-Crotalus_atrox_-Museum_of_Osteology%2C_Oklahoma_City%2C_Oklahoma%2C_USA-29Aug2012.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crotalus_atrox_-Museum_of_Osteology,_Oklahoma_City,_Oklahoma,_USA-29Aug2012.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skeleton of a <a href="/wiki/Diamondback_rattlesnake" title="Diamondback rattlesnake">diamondback rattlesnake</a></div></div></div>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">Reptiles</a></b> are a class of <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a> comprising <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">turtles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuatara" title="Tuatara">tuataras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a>. They are <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a>, but the snakes and a few species of lizard either have no limbs or their limbs are much reduced in size. Their bones are better ossified and their skeletons stronger than those of amphibians. The teeth are conical and mostly uniform in size. The surface cells of the epidermis are modified into horny scales which create a waterproof layer. Reptiles are unable to use their skin for respiration as do amphibians and have a more efficient respiratory system drawing air into their <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a> by expanding their chest walls. The heart resembles that of the amphibian but there is a septum which more completely separates the oxygenated and deoxygenated bloodstreams. The reproductive system has evolved for internal fertilization, with a <a href="/wiki/Sex_organ" title="Sex organ">copulatory organ</a> present in most species. The eggs are surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">amniotic membranes</a> which prevents them from drying out and are laid on land, or <a href="/wiki/Ovoviviparity" title="Ovoviviparity">develop internally</a> in some species. The bladder is small as nitrogenous waste is excreted as <a href="/wiki/Uric_acid" title="Uric acid">uric acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit865_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit865-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Turtles</b> are notable for their protective shells. They have an inflexible trunk encased in a horny <a href="/wiki/Carapace" title="Carapace">carapace</a> above and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plastron" title="Plastron">plastron</a> below. These are formed from bony plates embedded in the dermis which are overlain by horny ones and are partially fused with the ribs and spine. The neck is long and flexible and the head and the legs can be drawn back inside the shell. Turtles are vegetarians and the typical reptile teeth have been replaced by sharp, horny plates. In aquatic species, the front legs are modified into flippers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit868_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit868-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Tuataras</b> superficially resemble lizards but the lineages diverged in the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> period. There is one living species, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sphenodon_punctatus" title="Sphenodon punctatus">Sphenodon punctatus</a></i>. The skull has two openings (fenestrae) on either side and the jaw is rigidly attached to the skull. There is one row of teeth in the lower jaw and this fits between the two rows in the upper jaw when the animal chews. The teeth are merely projections of bony material from the jaw and eventually wear down. The brain and heart are more primitive than those of other reptiles, and the lungs have a single chamber and lack <a href="/wiki/Bronchus" title="Bronchus">bronchi</a>. The tuatara has a well-developed <a href="/wiki/Parietal_eye" title="Parietal eye">parietal eye</a> on its forehead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit868_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit868-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Lizards</b> have skulls with only one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasal_fenestra" title="Nasal fenestra">fenestra</a> on each side, the lower bar of bone below the second fenestra having been lost. This results in the jaws being less rigidly attached which allows the mouth to open wider. Lizards are mostly quadrupeds, with the trunk held off the ground by short, sideways-facing legs, but a few species have no limbs and resemble snakes. Lizards have moveable eyelids, eardrums are present and some species have a central parietal eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit868_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit868-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Snakes</b> are closely related to lizards, having branched off from a common ancestral lineage during the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> period, and they share many of the same features. The skeleton consists of a skull, a hyoid bone, spine and ribs though a few species retain a vestige of the pelvis and rear limbs in the form of <a href="/wiki/Pelvic_spur" title="Pelvic spur">pelvic spurs</a>. The bar under the second fenestra has also been lost and the jaws have extreme flexibility allowing the snake to swallow its prey whole. Snakes lack moveable eyelids, the eyes being covered by transparent "spectacle" scales. They do not have eardrums but can detect ground vibrations through the bones of their skull. Their forked tongues are used as organs of taste and smell and some species have sensory pits on their heads enabling them to locate warm-blooded prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit870_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit870-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crocodilians" title="Crocodilians">Crocodilians</a></b> are large, low-slung aquatic reptiles with long snouts and large numbers of teeth. The head and trunk are dorso-ventrally flattened and the tail is laterally compressed. It undulates from side to side to force the animal through the water when swimming. The tough keratinized scales provide body armour and some are fused to the skull. The nostrils, eyes and ears are elevated above the top of the flat head enabling them to remain above the surface of the water when the animal is floating. Valves seal the nostrils and ears when it is submerged. Unlike other reptiles, crocodilians have hearts with four chambers allowing complete separation of oxygenated and deoxygenated blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit874_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit874-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bird_anatomy">Bird anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Bird anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bird_anatomy" title="Bird anatomy">Bird anatomy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Duerer_wing_of_a_blue_roller.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1642" data-file-width="1695" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Duerer_wing_of_a_blue_roller.jpg/220px-Duerer_wing_of_a_blue_roller.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Duerer_wing_of_a_blue_roller.jpg/330px-Duerer_wing_of_a_blue_roller.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Duerer_wing_of_a_blue_roller.jpg/440px-Duerer_wing_of_a_blue_roller.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Duerer_wing_of_a_blue_roller.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Part of a wing. <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer" title="Albrecht Dürer">Albrecht Dürer</a>, c. 1500–1512</div></div></div>
<p>Birds are <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a> but though their hind limbs are used for walking or hopping, their front limbs are <a href="/wiki/Wing" title="Wing">wings</a> covered with <a href="/wiki/Feather" title="Feather">feathers</a> and adapted for flight. Birds are <a href="/wiki/Endotherm" title="Endotherm">endothermic</a>, have a high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metabolic_rate" title="Metabolic rate">metabolic rate</a>, a light <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeletal system</a> and powerful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscles</a>. The long bones are thin, hollow and very light. Air sac extensions from the lungs occupy the centre of some bones. The sternum is wide and usually has a keel and the caudal vertebrae are fused. There are no teeth and the narrow jaws are adapted into a horn-covered beak. The eyes are relatively large, particularly in nocturnal species such as owls. They face forwards in predators and sideways in ducks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit895_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit895-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>The feathers are outgrowths of the <a href="/wiki/Epidermis_(zoology)" title="Epidermis (zoology)">epidermis</a> and are found in localized bands from where they fan out over the skin. Large flight feathers are found on the wings and tail, contour feathers cover the bird's surface and fine down occurs on young birds and under the contour feathers of water birds. The only cutaneous gland is the single <a href="/wiki/Uropygial_gland" title="Uropygial gland">uropygial gland</a> near the base of the tail. This produces an oily secretion that waterproofs the feathers when the bird <a href="/wiki/Personal_grooming" title="Personal grooming">preens</a>. There are scales on the legs, feet and claws on the tips of the toes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit895_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit895-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mammal_anatomy">Mammal anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mammal anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammal_anatomy" title="Mammal anatomy">Mammal anatomy</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">Mammals</a> are a diverse class of animals, mostly terrestrial but some are aquatic and others have evolved flapping or gliding flight. They mostly have four limbs, but some aquatic mammals have no limbs or limbs modified into fins, and the forelimbs of bats are modified into wings. The legs of most mammals are situated below the trunk, which is held well clear of the ground. The bones of mammals are well ossified and their teeth, which are usually differentiated, are coated in a layer of <a href="/wiki/Tooth_enamel" title="Tooth enamel">prismatic enamel</a>. The teeth are shed once (<a href="/wiki/Deciduous_teeth" title="Deciduous teeth">milk teeth</a>) during the animal's lifetime or not at all, as is the case in <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" title="Cetacea">cetaceans</a>. Mammals have three bones in the middle ear and a <a href="/wiki/Cochlea" title="Cochlea">cochlea</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Inner_ear" title="Inner ear">inner ear</a>. They are clothed in hair and their skin contains glands which secrete <a href="/wiki/Sweat_gland" title="Sweat gland">sweat</a>. Some of these glands are specialized as <a href="/wiki/Mammary_gland" title="Mammary gland">mammary glands</a>, producing milk to feed the young. Mammals breathe with <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a> and have a muscular <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_diaphragm" title="Thoracic diaphragm">diaphragm</a> separating the thorax from the abdomen which helps them draw air into the lungs. The mammalian heart has four chambers, and oxygenated and deoxygenated blood are kept entirely separate. Nitrogenous waste is excreted primarily as urea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit914_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit914-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mammals are <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">amniotes</a>, and most are <a href="/wiki/Viviparity" title="Viviparity">viviparous</a>, giving birth to live young. Exceptions to this are the egg-laying <a href="/wiki/Monotreme" title="Monotreme">monotremes</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Platypus" title="Platypus">platypus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Echidna" title="Echidna">echidnas</a> of Australia. Most other mammals have a <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a> through which the developing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foetus" title="Foetus">foetus</a> obtains nourishment, but in <a href="/wiki/Marsupial" title="Marsupial">marsupials</a>, the foetal stage is very short and the immature young is born and finds its way to its mother's <a href="/wiki/Pouch_(marsupial)" title="Pouch (marsupial)">pouch</a> where it latches on to a <a href="/wiki/Nipple" title="Nipple">nipple</a> and completes its development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit914_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit914-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_anatomy">Human anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Human anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Human_body#Anatomy" title="Human body">Human body § Anatomy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_human_anatomy" title="Outline of human anatomy">Outline of human anatomy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parasagittal_MRI_of_human_head_in_patient_with_benign_familial_macrocephaly_prior_to_brain_injury_(ANIMATED).gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="256" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Parasagittal_MRI_of_human_head_in_patient_with_benign_familial_macrocephaly_prior_to_brain_injury_%28ANIMATED%29.gif/220px-Parasagittal_MRI_of_human_head_in_patient_with_benign_familial_macrocephaly_prior_to_brain_injury_%28ANIMATED%29.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/56/Parasagittal_MRI_of_human_head_in_patient_with_benign_familial_macrocephaly_prior_to_brain_injury_%28ANIMATED%29.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parasagittal_MRI_of_human_head_in_patient_with_benign_familial_macrocephaly_prior_to_brain_injury_(ANIMATED).gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Modern anatomic technique showing sagittal sections of the head as seen by an <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">MRI</a> scan</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Violin_Scroll_(2769099541).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2986" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Violin_Scroll_%282769099541%29.jpg/170px-Violin_Scroll_%282769099541%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Violin_Scroll_%282769099541%29.jpg/255px-Violin_Scroll_%282769099541%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Violin_Scroll_%282769099541%29.jpg/340px-Violin_Scroll_%282769099541%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Violin_Scroll_(2769099541).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the human, the development of skilled hand movements and increased brain size is likely to have evolved simultaneously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritBrit-Hand_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritBrit-Hand-39">[39]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">Humans</a> have the overall body plan of a mammal. Humans have a head, neck, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trunk_(anatomy)" title="Trunk (anatomy)">trunk</a> (which includes the <a href="/wiki/Thorax" title="Thorax">thorax</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">abdomen</a>), two arms and hands, and two legs and feet.
</p><p>Generally, students of certain <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biological sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paramedic" title="Paramedic">paramedics</a>, prosthetists and orthotists, <a href="/wiki/Physical_therapy" title="Physical therapy">physiotherapists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Occupational_therapy" title="Occupational therapy">occupational therapists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nursing" title="Nursing">nurses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Podiatry" title="Podiatry">podiatrists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Medical_school" title="Medical school">medical students</a> learn gross anatomy and microscopic anatomy from anatomical models, skeletons, textbooks, diagrams, photographs, lectures and tutorials and in addition, medical students generally also learn gross anatomy through practical experience of <a href="/wiki/Dissection" title="Dissection">dissection</a> and inspection of <a href="/wiki/Cadaver" title="Cadaver">cadavers</a>. The study of microscopic anatomy (or <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">histology</a>) can be aided by practical experience examining histological preparations (or slides) under a <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Human anatomy, physiology and biochemistry are complementary basic medical sciences, which are generally taught to medical students in their first year at medical school. Human anatomy can be taught regionally or systemically; that is, respectively, studying anatomy by bodily regions such as the head and chest, or studying by specific systems, such as the nervous or respiratory systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_HGray_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-intro_HGray-3">[3]</a></sup> The major anatomy textbook, <a href="/wiki/Gray%27s_Anatomy" title="Gray's Anatomy">Gray's Anatomy</a>, has been reorganized from a systems format to a regional format, in line with modern teaching methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> A thorough working knowledge of anatomy is required by physicians, especially <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">surgeons</a> and doctors working in some diagnostic specialties, such as <a href="/wiki/Histopathology" title="Histopathology">histopathology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radiology" title="Radiology">radiology</a>.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAA_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAA-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Academic anatomists are usually employed by universities, medical schools or teaching hospitals. They are often involved in teaching anatomy, and research into certain systems, organs, tissues or cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAA_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-AAA-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Invertebrate_anatomy">Invertebrate anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Invertebrate anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chirocephalus_diaphanus_male_head.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="568" data-file-width="708" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Chirocephalus_diaphanus_male_head.png/220px-Chirocephalus_diaphanus_male_head.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Chirocephalus_diaphanus_male_head.png/330px-Chirocephalus_diaphanus_male_head.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Chirocephalus_diaphanus_male_head.png/440px-Chirocephalus_diaphanus_male_head.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chirocephalus_diaphanus_male_head.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Head of a male <i><a href="/wiki/Daphnia" title="Daphnia">Daphnia</a></i>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planktonic" title="Planktonic">planktonic</a> crustacean</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">Invertebrates</a> constitute a vast array of living organisms ranging from the simplest unicellular <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Paramecium" title="Paramecium">Paramecium</a></i> to such complex multicellular animals as the <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lobster" title="Lobster">lobster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly" title="Dragonfly">dragonfly</a>. They constitute about 95% of the animal species. By definition, none of these creatures has a backbone. The cells of single-cell <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">protozoans</a> have the same basic structure as those of multicellular animals but some parts are specialized into the equivalent of tissues and organs. Locomotion is often provided by <a href="/wiki/Cilium" title="Cilium">cilia</a> or <a href="/wiki/Flagellum" title="Flagellum">flagella</a> or may proceed via the advance of <a href="/wiki/Pseudopodia" title="Pseudopodia">pseudopodia</a>, food may be gathered by <a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis" title="Phagocytosis">phagocytosis</a>, energy needs may be supplied by <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> and the cell may be supported by an <a href="/wiki/Endoskeleton" title="Endoskeleton">endoskeleton</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">exoskeleton</a>. Some protozoans can form multicellular colonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metazoa" title="Metazoa">Metazoans</a> are a multicellular organism, with different groups of cells serving different functions. The most basic types of metazoan tissues are epithelium and connective tissue, both of which are present in nearly all invertebrates. The outer surface of the epidermis is normally formed of epithelial cells and secretes an <a href="/wiki/Extracellular_matrix" title="Extracellular matrix">extracellular matrix</a> which provides support to the organism. An endoskeleton derived from the <a href="/wiki/Mesoderm" title="Mesoderm">mesoderm</a> is present in <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">echinoderms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">sponges</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a>. <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">Exoskeletons</a> are derived from the epidermis and is composed of <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> (insects, spiders, ticks, shrimps, crabs, lobsters). <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">Calcium carbonate</a> constitutes the shells of <a href="/wiki/Mollusca" title="Mollusca">molluscs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brachiopod" title="Brachiopod">brachiopods</a> and some tube-building <a href="/wiki/Polychaete" title="Polychaete">polychaete worms</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a> forms the exoskeleton of the microscopic <a href="/wiki/Diatom" title="Diatom">diatoms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radiolaria" title="Radiolaria">radiolaria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Other invertebrates may have no rigid structures but the epidermis may secrete a variety of surface coatings such as the <a href="/wiki/Pinacoderm" title="Pinacoderm">pinacoderm</a> of sponges, the gelatinous cuticle of cnidarians (<a href="/wiki/Polyp_(zoology)" title="Polyp (zoology)">polyps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_anemone" title="Sea anemone">sea anemones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagenous</a> cuticle of <a href="/wiki/Annelid" title="Annelid">annelids</a>. The outer epithelial layer may include cells of several types including sensory cells, gland cells and stinging cells. There may also be protrusions such as <a href="/wiki/Microvillus" title="Microvillus">microvilli</a>, cilia, bristles, <a href="/wiki/Spine_(zoology)" title="Spine (zoology)">spines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tubercle" title="Tubercle">tubercles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Marcello_Malpighi" title="Marcello Malpighi">Marcello Malpighi</a>, the father of microscopical anatomy, discovered that plants had tubules similar to those he saw in insects like the silk worm. He observed that when a ring-like portion of bark was removed on a trunk a swelling occurred in the tissues above the ring, and he unmistakably interpreted this as growth stimulated by food coming down from the leaves, and being captured above the ring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArberAgnes_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArberAgnes-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arthropod_anatomy">Arthropod anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Arthropod anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">Arthropod</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insect_morphology" title="Insect morphology">Insect morphology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spider_anatomy" title="Spider anatomy">Spider anatomy</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">Arthropods</a> comprise the largest phylum in the animal kingdom with over a million known invertebrate species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">Insects</a> possess <a href="/wiki/Segmentation_(biology)" title="Segmentation (biology)">segmented</a> bodies supported by a hard-jointed outer covering, the <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">exoskeleton</a>, made mostly of chitin. The segments of the body are organized into three distinct parts, a head, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorax_(insect_anatomy)" title="Thorax (insect anatomy)">thorax</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">abdomen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The head typically bears a pair of sensory <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(biology)" title="Antenna (biology)">antennae</a>, a pair of <a href="/wiki/Compound_eye" title="Compound eye">compound eyes</a>, one to three simple eyes (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocelli" title="Ocelli">ocelli</a>) and three sets of modified appendages that form the <a href="/wiki/Insect_mouthparts" title="Insect mouthparts">mouthparts</a>. The thorax has three pairs of segmented <a href="/wiki/Arthropod_leg" title="Arthropod leg">legs</a>, one pair each for the three segments that compose the thorax and one or two pairs of <a href="/wiki/Insect_wing" title="Insect wing">wings</a>. The abdomen is composed of eleven segments, some of which may be fused and houses the <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Respiration_(physiology)" title="Respiration (physiology)">respiratory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Excretion" title="Excretion">excretory</a> and reproductive systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gullan_and_Cranston_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gullan_and_Cranston-50">[50]</a></sup> There is considerable variation between species and many adaptations to the body parts, especially wings, legs, antennae and mouthparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">Spiders</a> a class of <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnids</a> have four pairs of legs; a body of two segments—a <a href="/wiki/Cephalothorax" title="Cephalothorax">cephalothorax</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">abdomen</a>. Spiders have no wings and no antennae. They have mouthparts called <a href="/wiki/Chelicerae" title="Chelicerae">chelicerae</a> which are often connected to venom glands as most spiders are venomous. They have a second pair of appendages called <a href="/wiki/Pedipalp" title="Pedipalp">pedipalps</a> attached to the cephalothorax. These have similar segmentation to the legs and function as taste and smell organs. At the end of each male pedipalp is a spoon-shaped cymbium that acts to support the <a href="/wiki/Palpal_bulb" title="Palpal bulb">copulatory organ</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_branches_of_anatomy">Other branches of anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other branches of anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superficial_anatomy" title="Superficial anatomy">Superficial or surface anatomy</a> is important as the study of anatomical landmarks that can be readily seen from the exterior contours of the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_HGray_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-intro_HGray-3">[3]</a></sup> It enables physicians or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veterinary_surgeon" title="Veterinary surgeon">veterinary surgeons</a> to gauge the position and anatomy of the associated deeper structures. Superficial is a directional term that indicates that structures are located relatively close to the surface of the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Comparative_anatomy" title="Comparative anatomy">Comparative anatomy</a> relates to the comparison of anatomical structures (both gross and microscopic) in different animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_HGray_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-intro_HGray-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li>Artistic anatomy relates to anatomic studies for artistic reasons.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_anatomy" title="History of anatomy">History of anatomy</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient">Ancient</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Ancient">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
