<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Foetal_positions_in_uterus,_pregnant_female_Wellcome_L0000845.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1634" data-file-width="1282" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Foetal_positions_in_uterus%2C_pregnant_female_Wellcome_L0000845.jpg/230px-Foetal_positions_in_uterus%2C_pregnant_female_Wellcome_L0000845.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Foetal_positions_in_uterus%2C_pregnant_female_Wellcome_L0000845.jpg/345px-Foetal_positions_in_uterus%2C_pregnant_female_Wellcome_L0000845.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Foetal_positions_in_uterus%2C_pregnant_female_Wellcome_L0000845.jpg/460px-Foetal_positions_in_uterus%2C_pregnant_female_Wellcome_L0000845.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Foetal_positions_in_uterus,_pregnant_female_Wellcome_L0000845.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Image of early rendition of anatomy findings</div></div></div>
<p>In 1600 BCE, the <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Smith_Papyrus" title="Edwin Smith Papyrus">Edwin Smith Papyrus</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_medicine" title="Ancient Egyptian medicine">Ancient Egyptian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_manual" title="Medical manual">medical text</a>, described the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>, its vessels, <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spleen" title="Spleen">spleen</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kidneys" title="Kidneys">kidneys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">uterus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urinary_bladder" title="Urinary bladder">bladder</a>, and showed the <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a> diverging from the heart. The <a href="/wiki/Ebers_Papyrus" title="Ebers Papyrus">Ebers Papyrus</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1550 BCE</span>) features a "treatise on the heart", with vessels carrying all the body's fluids to or from every member of the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ancient Greek anatomy and physiology underwent great changes and advances throughout the early medieval world.  Over time, this medical practice expanded by a continually developing understanding of the functions of organs and structures in the body. Phenomenal anatomical observations of the human body were made, which have contributed towards the understanding of the brain, eye, liver, reproductive organs and the nervous system.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Egypt" title="Hellenistic Egypt">Hellenistic Egyptian</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> was the stepping-stone for Greek anatomy and physiology. Alexandria not only housed the biggest library for medical records and books of the liberal arts in the world during the time of the Greeks, but was also home to many medical practitioners and philosophers. Great patronage of the arts and sciences from the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> rulers helped raise Alexandria up, further rivalling the cultural and scientific achievements of other Greek states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Longrigg_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Longrigg-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Blue_Beryl-Anatomy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2080" data-file-width="1648" decoding="async" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/The_Blue_Beryl-Anatomy.jpg/220px-The_Blue_Beryl-Anatomy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/The_Blue_Beryl-Anatomy.jpg/330px-The_Blue_Beryl-Anatomy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/The_Blue_Beryl-Anatomy.jpg/440px-The_Blue_Beryl-Anatomy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Blue_Beryl-Anatomy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An anatomy <a href="/wiki/Thangka" title="Thangka">thangka</a>, part of <a href="/wiki/Desi_Sangye_Gyatso" title="Desi Sangye Gyatso">Desi Sangye Gyatso</a>'s The Blue Beryl, 17th century</div></div></div>
<p>Some of the most striking advances in early anatomy and physiology took place in Hellenistic Alexandria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Longrigg_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Longrigg-54">[54]</a></sup> Two of the most famous anatomists and physiologists of the third century were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herophilus" title="Herophilus">Herophilus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erasistratus" title="Erasistratus">Erasistratus</a>. These two physicians helped pioneer human <a href="/wiki/Dissection" title="Dissection">dissection</a> for medical research. They also conducted <a href="/wiki/Vivisection" title="Vivisection">vivisections</a> on the cadavers of condemned criminals, which was considered taboo until the Renaissance—Herophilus was recognized as the first person to perform systematic dissections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Herophilus became known for his anatomical works making impressing contributions to many branches of anatomy and many other aspects of medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Some of the works included classifying the system of the pulse, the discovery that human arteries had thicker walls than veins, and that the atria were parts of the heart. Herophilus's knowledge of the human body has provided vital input towards understanding the brain, eye, liver, reproductive organs and nervous system, and characterizing the course of disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Erasistratus accurately described the structure of the brain, including the cavities and membranes, and made a distinction between its cerebrum and cerebellum<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> During his study in Alexandria, Erasistratus was particularly concerned with studies of the circulatory and nervous systems. He was able to distinguish the sensory and the motor nerves in the human body and believed that air entered the lungs and heart, which was then carried throughout the body. His distinction between the arteries and veins—the arteries carrying the air through the body, while the veins carried the blood from the heart was a great anatomical discovery. Erasistratus was also responsible for naming and describing the function of the epiglottis and the valves of the heart, including the tricuspid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> During the third century, Greek physicians were able to differentiate nerves from blood vessels and tendons<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> and to realize that the nerves convey neural impulses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Longrigg_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-Longrigg-54">[54]</a></sup> It was Herophilus who made the point that damage to motor nerves induced paralysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Herophilus named the meninges and ventricles in the brain, appreciated the division between cerebellum and cerebrum and recognized that the brain was the "seat of intellect" and not a "cooling chamber" as propounded by Aristotle<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Herophilus is also credited with describing the optic, oculomotor, motor division of the trigeminal, facial, vestibulocochlear and hypoglossal nerves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_University_Press_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_University_Press-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zahrawi1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="305" decoding="async" height="313" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Zahrawi1.png/200px-Zahrawi1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Zahrawi1.png/300px-Zahrawi1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dc/Zahrawi1.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zahrawi1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Surgical instruments were invented for the first time in history by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abulcasis" title="Abulcasis">Abulcasis</a> in the 11th century</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cheshm_manuscript.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/200px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/300px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/400px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cheshm_manuscript.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anatomy of the eye for the first time in history by <a href="/wiki/Hunayn_ibn_Ishaq" title="Hunayn ibn Ishaq">Hunayn ibn Ishaq</a> in the 9th century</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:13th_century_anatomical_illustration.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1264" data-file-width="872" decoding="async" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/13th_century_anatomical_illustration.jpg/220px-13th_century_anatomical_illustration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/13th_century_anatomical_illustration.jpg/330px-13th_century_anatomical_illustration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/13th_century_anatomical_illustration.jpg/440px-13th_century_anatomical_illustration.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:13th_century_anatomical_illustration.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>13th century anatomical illustration</div></div></div>
<p>Great feats were made during the third century BCE in both the digestive and reproductive systems. Herophilus was able to discover and describe not only the salivary glands, but the small intestine and liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_University_Press_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_University_Press-63">[63]</a></sup> He showed that the uterus is a hollow organ and described the ovaries and uterine tubes. He recognized that spermatozoa were produced by the testes and was the first to identify the prostate gland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_University_Press_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_University_Press-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The anatomy of the muscles and skeleton is described in the <i><a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Corpus" title="Hippocratic Corpus">Hippocratic Corpus</a></i>, an Ancient Greek medical work written by unknown authors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> described <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> anatomy based on animal <a href="/wiki/Dissection" title="Dissection">dissection</a>. <a href="/wiki/Praxagoras" title="Praxagoras">Praxagoras</a> identified the difference between <a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">arteries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">veins</a>. Also in the 4th century BCE, <a href="/wiki/Herophilos" title="Herophilos">Herophilos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erasistratus" title="Erasistratus">Erasistratus</a> produced more accurate anatomical descriptions based on <a href="/wiki/Vivisection" title="Vivisection">vivisection</a> of criminals in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodies_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodies-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 2nd century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galen_of_Pergamum" title="Galen of Pergamum">Galen of Pergamum</a>, an anatomist, <a href="/wiki/Clinician" title="Clinician">clinician</a>, writer and philosopher,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritBrit-Galen_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritBrit-Galen-67">[67]</a></sup> wrote the final and highly influential anatomy treatise of ancient times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid1081972_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid1081972-68">[68]</a></sup> He compiled existing knowledge and studied anatomy through dissection of animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritBrit-Galen_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-BritBrit-Galen-67">[67]</a></sup> He was one of the first experimental physiologists through his <a href="/wiki/Vivisection" title="Vivisection">vivisection</a> experiments on animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Galen's drawings, based mostly on dog anatomy, became effectively the only anatomical textbook for the next thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boas_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boas-70">[70]</a></sup> His work was known to <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> doctors only through <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a> medicine until it was translated from the Greek some time in the 15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boas_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boas-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_to_early_modern">Medieval to early modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Medieval to early modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_RCIN_919000,_Verso_The_bones_and_muscles_of_the_arm_c.1510-11.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1676" data-file-width="1168" decoding="async" height="316" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_RCIN_919000%2C_Verso_The_bones_and_muscles_of_the_arm_c.1510-11.jpg/220px-Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_RCIN_919000%2C_Verso_The_bones_and_muscles_of_the_arm_c.1510-11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_RCIN_919000%2C_Verso_The_bones_and_muscles_of_the_arm_c.1510-11.jpg/330px-Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_RCIN_919000%2C_Verso_The_bones_and_muscles_of_the_arm_c.1510-11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_RCIN_919000%2C_Verso_The_bones_and_muscles_of_the_arm_c.1510-11.jpg/440px-Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_RCIN_919000%2C_Verso_The_bones_and_muscles_of_the_arm_c.1510-11.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leonardo_da_Vinci_-_RCIN_919000,_Verso_The_bones_and_muscles_of_the_arm_c.1510-11.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anatomical study of the arm, by <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>, (about 1510)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit,_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam,_1543..JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="530" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit%2C_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam%2C_1543..JPG/170px-Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit%2C_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam%2C_1543..JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit%2C_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam%2C_1543..JPG/255px-Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit%2C_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam%2C_1543..JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit%2C_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam%2C_1543..JPG/340px-Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit%2C_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam%2C_1543..JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit,_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam,_1543..JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anatomical chart in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesalius" title="Vesalius">Vesalius</a>'s <i>Epitome</i>, 1543</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Michiel_Jansz_van_Mierevelt_-_Anatomy_lesson_of_Dr._Willem_van_der_Meer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="351" data-file-width="470" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Michiel_Jansz_van_Mierevelt_-_Anatomy_lesson_of_Dr._Willem_van_der_Meer.jpg/220px-Michiel_Jansz_van_Mierevelt_-_Anatomy_lesson_of_Dr._Willem_van_der_Meer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Michiel_Jansz_van_Mierevelt_-_Anatomy_lesson_of_Dr._Willem_van_der_Meer.jpg/330px-Michiel_Jansz_van_Mierevelt_-_Anatomy_lesson_of_Dr._Willem_van_der_Meer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Michiel_Jansz_van_Mierevelt_-_Anatomy_lesson_of_Dr._Willem_van_der_Meer.jpg/440px-Michiel_Jansz_van_Mierevelt_-_Anatomy_lesson_of_Dr._Willem_van_der_Meer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Michiel_Jansz_van_Mierevelt_-_Anatomy_lesson_of_Dr._Willem_van_der_Meer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michiel_Jansz_van_Mierevelt" title="Michiel Jansz van Mierevelt">Michiel Jansz van Mierevelt</a> – <i>Anatomy lesson of Dr. Willem van der Meer</i>, 1617</div></div></div>
<p>Anatomy developed little from classical times until the sixteenth century; as the historian Marie Boas writes, "Progress in anatomy before the sixteenth century is as mysteriously slow as its development after 1500 is startlingly rapid".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boas_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Boas-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 120–121">: 120–121 </span></sup> Between 1275 and 1326, the anatomists <a href="/wiki/Mondino_de_Luzzi" title="Mondino de Luzzi">Mondino de Luzzi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Achillini" title="Alessandro Achillini">Alessandro Achillini</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Benivieni" title="Antonio Benivieni">Antonio Benivieni</a> at <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a> carried out the first systematic human dissections since ancient times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZimmermanVeith1993_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZimmermanVeith1993-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crombie1959_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crombie1959-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thorndike1958_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thorndike1958-73">[73]</a></sup> Mondino's <i>Anatomy</i> of 1316 was the first textbook in the medieval rediscovery of human anatomy. It describes the body in the order followed in Mondino's dissections, starting with the abdomen, then the thorax, then the head and limbs. It was the standard anatomy textbook for the next century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boas_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-Boas-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> (1452–1519) was trained in anatomy by <a href="/wiki/Andrea_del_Verrocchio" title="Andrea del Verrocchio">Andrea del Verrocchio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boas_70-4"><a href="#cite_note-Boas-70">[70]</a></sup> He made use of his anatomical knowledge in his artwork, making many sketches of skeletal structures, muscles and organs of humans and other vertebrates that he dissected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boas_70-5"><a href="#cite_note-Boas-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Andreas_Vesalius" title="Andreas Vesalius">Andreas Vesalius</a> (1514–1564), professor of anatomy at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Padua" title="University of Padua">University of Padua</a>, is considered the founder of modern human anatomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Originally from <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Brabant" title="Duchy of Brabant">Brabant</a>, Vesalius published the influential book <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_humani_corporis_fabrica" title="De humani corporis fabrica">De humani corporis fabrica</a></i> ("the structure of the human body"), a large format book in seven volumes, in 1543.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The accurate and intricately detailed illustrations, often in <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegorical</a> poses against Italianate landscapes, are thought to have been made by the artist <a href="/wiki/Jan_van_Calcar" title="Jan van Calcar">Jan van Calcar</a>, a pupil of <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>In England, anatomy was the subject of the first public lectures given in any science; these were given by the <a href="/wiki/Barber_surgeon" title="Barber surgeon">Company of Barbers and Surgeons</a> in the 16th century, joined in 1583 by the Lumleian lectures in surgery at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Physicians" title="Royal College of Physicians">Royal College of Physicians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boas229_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boas229-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_modern">Late modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatomy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Late modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_anatomy_in_the_19th_century" title="History of anatomy in the 19th century">History of anatomy in the 19th century</a></div>
<p>In the United States, medical schools began to be set up towards the end of the 18th century. Classes in anatomy needed a continual stream of cadavers for dissection and these were difficult to obtain. Philadelphia, Baltimore and New York were all renowned for <a href="/wiki/Body_snatching" title="Body snatching">body snatching</a> activity as criminals raided graveyards at night, removing newly buried corpses from their coffins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trafficdead_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-trafficdead-79">[79]</a></sup> A similar problem existed in Britain where demand for bodies became so great that grave-raiding and even <a href="/wiki/Anatomy_murder" title="Anatomy murder">anatomy murder</a> were practised to obtain cadavers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosner,_Lisa._2010_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosner,_Lisa._2010-80">[80]</a></sup> Some graveyards were in consequence protected with watchtowers. The practice was halted in Britain by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomy_Act" title="Anatomy Act">Anatomy Act</a> of 1832,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> while in the United States, similar legislation was enacted after the physician <a href="/wiki/William_S._Forbes" title="William S. Forbes">William S. Forbes</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jefferson_Medical_College" title="Jefferson Medical College">Jefferson Medical College</a> was found guilty in 1882 of "complicity with resurrectionists in the despoliation of graves in Lebanon Cemetery".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>The teaching of anatomy in Britain was transformed by Sir <a href="/wiki/John_Struthers_(anatomist)" title="John Struthers (anatomist)">John Struthers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regius_Professor_of_Anatomy_(Aberdeen)" title="Regius Professor of Anatomy (Aberdeen)">Regius Professor of Anatomy</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Aberdeen" title="University of Aberdeen">University of Aberdeen</a> from 1863 to 1889. He was responsible for setting up the system of three years of "pre-clinical" academic teaching in the sciences underlying medicine, including especially anatomy. This system lasted until the reform of medical training in 1993 and 2003. As well as teaching, he collected many vertebrate skeletons for his museum of <a href="/wiki/Comparative_anatomy" title="Comparative anatomy">comparative anatomy</a>, published over 70 research papers, and became famous for his public dissection of the <a href="/wiki/Tay_Whale" title="Tay Whale">Tay Whale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17373426_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17373426-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15712576_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15712576-85">[85]</a></sup> From 1822 the Royal College of Surgeons regulated the teaching of anatomy in medical schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLachlan,_J._2006._p.243-53_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLachlan,_J._2006._p.243-53-86">[86]</a></sup> Medical museums provided examples in comparative anatomy, and were often used in teaching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_Semmelweis" title="Ignaz Semmelweis">Ignaz Semmelweis</a> investigated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerperal_fever" title="Puerperal fever">puerperal fever</a> and he discovered how it was caused. He noticed that the frequently fatal fever occurred more often in mothers examined by medical students than by midwives. The students went from the dissecting room to the hospital ward and examined women in childbirth. Semmelweis showed that when the trainees washed their hands in chlorinated lime before each clinical examination, the incidence of puerperal fever among the mothers could be reduced dramatically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="454" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg/170px-Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg/255px-Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg/340px-Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siemens-electron-microscope.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An electron microscope from 1973</div></div></div>
<p>Before the modern medical era, the main means for studying the internal structures of the body were <a href="/wiki/Dissection" title="Dissection">dissection</a> of the dead and <a href="/wiki/Inspection" title="Inspection">inspection</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palpation" title="Palpation">palpation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Auscultation" title="Auscultation">auscultation</a> of the living. It was the advent of <a href="/wiki/Microscopy" title="Microscopy">microscopy</a> that opened up an understanding of the building blocks that constituted living tissues. Technical advances in the development of <a href="/wiki/Achromatic_lens" title="Achromatic lens">achromatic lenses</a> increased the <a href="/wiki/Angular_resolution" title="Angular resolution">resolving power</a> of the microscope and around 1839, <a href="/wiki/Matthias_Jakob_Schleiden" title="Matthias Jakob Schleiden">Matthias Jakob Schleiden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Schwann" title="Theodor Schwann">Theodor Schwann</a> identified that cells were the fundamental unit of organization of all living things. Study of small structures involved passing light through them and the <a href="/wiki/Microtome" title="Microtome">microtome</a> was invented to provide sufficiently thin slices of tissue to examine. Staining techniques using artificial dyes were established to help distinguish between different types of tissue. Advances in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">histology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cell_biology" title="Cell biology">cytology</a> began in the late 19th century<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritMicro_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritMicro-89">[89]</a></sup> along with advances in surgical techniques allowing for the painless and safe removal of <a href="/wiki/Biopsy" title="Biopsy">biopsy</a> specimens. The invention of the <a href="/wiki/Electron_microscope" title="Electron microscope">electron microscope</a> brought a great advance in resolution power and allowed research into the <a href="/wiki/Ultrastructure" title="Ultrastructure">ultrastructure</a> of cells and the <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a> and other structures within them. About the same time, in the 1950s, the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_diffraction" title="X-ray diffraction">X-ray diffraction</a> for studying the crystal structures of proteins, nucleic acids and other biological molecules gave rise to a new field of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_anatomy" title="Molecular anatomy">molecular anatomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritMicro_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-BritMicro-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Equally important advances have occurred in  <i>non-invasive</i> techniques for examining the interior structures of the body. <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> can be passed through the body and used in medical <a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">radiography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fluoroscopy" title="Fluoroscopy">fluoroscopy</a> to differentiate interior structures that have varying degrees of opaqueness. <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">Magnetic resonance imaging</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_computed_tomography" title="X-ray computed tomography">computed tomography</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_ultrasonography" title="Medical ultrasonography">ultrasound imaging</a> have all enabled examination of internal structures in unprecedented detail to a degree far beyond the imagination of earlier generations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
